   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Mandarin Chinese
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       This article is about the entire group of Mandarin Chinese language varieties. For Standard Mandarin, the official variety, see
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Standard Chinese
       </a>
       . For the administrative language of China during the Ming and Qing dynasties, see
       <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">
        Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">

         </span>

         </a>
         <div>
          Guānhuà (
          <i>
           Mandarin
          </i>
          )
          <br/>
          written in
          <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">
           Chinese characters
          </a>
          <br/>
          (traditional Chinese on the left, simplified Chinese on the right)
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>

         Region
        </th>

         Most of northern and southwestern China
         <small>
          (see also
          <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
           Standard Chinese
          </a>
          )
         </small>
        </td>
       </tr>

         </div>
        </th>
        <td style="line-height:1.35em;">

           [1]

         <span style="font-size: 1rem">
          <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
           官话
          </span>
         </span>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

            <sup>

       <tr style="display:none">
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Mandarin
       </b>
       (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'m' in 'my'">
            m
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          , -
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'d' in 'dye'">
            d
           </span>
           <span title="'r' in 'rye'">
            r
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
        <small class="nowrap">
         (
         <span class="unicode haudio">
          <span class="fn">
           <span style="white-space:nowrap">
            <a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Mandarin.ogg" title="About this sound">
             <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
            </a>
           </span>
           <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/En-us-Mandarin.ogg" title="En-us-Mandarin.ogg">
            listen
           </a>
          </span>
         </span>
         )
        </small>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">
        simplified Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%98%E8%AF%9D" title="wikt:官话">
         官话
        </a>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">
        traditional Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%AE%98%E8%A9%B1" title="wikt:官話">
         官話
        </a>
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         Guānhuà
        </span>
       </i>
       ; literally: "speech of officials") is a group of related
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">
        varieties of Chinese
       </a>
       spoken across most of northern and southwestern
       <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">
        China
       </a>
       . The group includes the
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">
        Beijing dialect
       </a>
       , the basis of
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Standard Mandarin or Standard Chinese
       </a>
       . Because most Mandarin dialects are found in the north, the group is sometimes referred to as the
       <b>
        Northern dialects
       </b>
       (
       <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
        北方话
       </span>
       ;
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         běifānghuà
        </span>
       </i>
       ). Many local Mandarin varieties are not
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mutually_intelligible" title="Mutually intelligible">
        mutually intelligible
       </a>
       . Nevertheless, Mandarin is often placed first in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lists_of_languages_by_number_of_native_speakers" title="Lists of languages by number of native speakers">
        lists of languages by number of native speakers
       </a>
       (with nearly a billion).
      </p>
      <p>
       Mandarin is by far the largest of the seven or ten Chinese dialect groups, spoken by 70 percent of all Chinese speakers over a large geographical area, stretching from
       <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">
        Yunnan
       </a>
       in the southwest to
       <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">
        Xinjiang
       </a>
       in the northwest and
       <a href="/wiki/Heilongjiang" title="Heilongjiang">
        Heilongjiang
       </a>
       in the northeast. This is generally attributed to the greater ease of travel and communication in the
       <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">
        North China Plain
       </a>
       compared to the more mountainous south, combined with the relatively recent spread of Mandarin to frontier areas.
      </p>
      <p>
       Most Mandarin varieties have four
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tones
       </a>
       . The final stops of
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">
        Middle Chinese
       </a>
       have disappeared in most of these varieties, but some have merged them as a final
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       . Many Mandarin varieties, including the Beijing dialect, retain
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retroflex" title="Retroflex">
        retroflex
       </a>
       initial consonants, which have been lost in southern dialect groups.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Historical_capitals_of_China" title="Historical capitals of China">
        capital
       </a>
       has been within the Mandarin area for most of the last millennium, making these dialects very influential. Some form of Mandarin has served as a national
       <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">
        lingua franca
       </a>
       since the 14th century. In the early 20th century, a standard form based on the Beijing dialect, with elements from other Mandarin dialects, was adopted as the
       <a href="/wiki/National_language" title="National language">
        national language
       </a>
       . Standard Chinese is the
       <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">
        official language
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">
        People's Republic of China
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Taiwan
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and one of the four official
       <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Singapore" title="Languages of Singapore">
        languages of Singapore
       </a>
       . It is used as one of the working languages of the United Nations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also one of the most frequently used varieties of Chinese among
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_diaspora" title="Chinese diaspora">
        Chinese diaspora
       </a>
       communities internationally.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The English word "mandarin" (from
       <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">
        Portuguese
       </a>
       <i>
        mandarim
       </i>
       , from
       <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">
        Malay
       </a>
       <i>
        menteri
       </i>
       , from
       <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">
        Sanskrit
       </a>
       <i>
        mantrin
       </i>
       , meaning "minister or counsellor") originally meant an
       <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(bureaucrat)" title="Mandarin (bureaucrat)">
        official
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_empire" title="Ming empire">
        Ming
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qing_empire" title="Qing empire">
        Qing
       </a>
       empires.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [a]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since their native varieties were often mutually unintelligible, these officials communicated using a
       <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">
        Koiné language
       </a>
       based on various northern varieties. When
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">
        Jesuit
       </a>
       missionaries learned this standard language in the 16th century, they called it "Mandarin", from its Chinese name
       <i>
        Guānhuà
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        官话/官話
       </span>
       ), or "language of the officials".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000537_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000537-11">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In everyday English, "Mandarin" refers to
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Standard Chinese
       </a>
       , which is often called simply "Chinese". Standard Chinese is based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">
        particular Mandarin dialect spoken
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       , with some lexical and syntactic influence from other Mandarin dialects. It is the official spoken language of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="People's Republic of China">
        People's Republic of China
       </a>
       (PRC), the official language of the
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Republic of China
       </a>
       (ROC, Taiwan), and one of the four official languages of the Republic of
       <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">
        Singapore
       </a>
       . It also functions as the language of instruction in Mainland China and in Taiwan. It is one of the six official languages of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">
        United Nations
       </a>
       , under the name "Chinese". Chinese speakers refer to the modern standard language as
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         Pǔtōnghuà
        </i>
        (
        <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
         普通话/普通話
        </span>
        , literally "common speech") in Mainland China,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Guóyǔ
        </i>
        (
        <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">
         國語
        </span>
        , literally "national language") in Taiwan, or
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Huáyǔ
        </i>
        (
        <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
         华语/華語
        </span>
        , literally "
        <a href="/wiki/Huaxia" title="Huaxia">
         Hua
        </a>
        language/Chinese Language") in Singapore, Malaysia, Indonesia and Philippines,
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       but not as
       <i>
        Guānhuà
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988136_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988136-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Linguists use the term "Mandarin" to refer to the diverse group of dialects spoken in northern and southwestern China, which Chinese linguists call
       <i>
        Guānhuà
       </i>
       . The alternative term
       <i>
        Běifānghuà
       </i>
       (
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        北方话/北方話
       </span>
       ), or "Northern dialects", is used less and less among Chinese linguists. By extension, the term "Old Mandarin" or "Early Mandarin" is used by linguists to refer to the northern dialects recorded in materials from the Yuan dynasty.
      </p>
      <p>
       Native speakers who are not academic linguists may not recognize that the variants they speak are classified in linguistics as members of "Mandarin" (or so-called "Northern dialects") in a broader sense. Within Chinese social or cultural discourse, there is not a common "Mandarin" identity based on language; rather, there are strong regional identities centred on individual dialects because of the wide geographical distribution and cultural diversity of their speakers. Speakers of forms of Mandarin other than the standard typically refer to the variety they speak by a geographic name—for example
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sichuanese_Mandarin" title="Sichuanese Mandarin">
        Sichuan dialect
       </a>
       , Hebei dialect or
       <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mandarin" title="Northeastern Mandarin">
        Northeastern dialect
       </a>
       , all being regarded as distinct from the standard language.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The hundreds of modern local
       <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">
        varieties of Chinese
       </a>
       developed from regional variants of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Chinese" title="Old Chinese">
        Old Chinese
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">
        Middle Chinese
       </a>
       . Traditionally, seven major groups of dialects have been recognized. Aside from Mandarin, the other six are
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">
        Wu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">
        Gan
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">
        Xiang
       </a>
       in central China, and
       <a href="/wiki/Min_Chinese" title="Min Chinese">
        Min
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hakka_Chinese" title="Hakka Chinese">
        Hakka
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yue_Chinese" title="Yue Chinese">
        Yue
       </a>
       on the southeast coast.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988181_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988181-13">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">
         Language Atlas of China
        </a>
       </i>
       (1987) distinguishes three further groups:
       <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">
        Jin
       </a>
       (split from Mandarin),
       <a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">
        Huizhou
       </a>
       in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huizhou_region" title="Huizhou region">
        Huizhou region
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">
        Anhui
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">
        Zhejiang
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pinghua" title="Pinghua">
        Pinghua
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">
        Guangxi
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">
        Yunnan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56-15">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Mandarin">
        Old Mandarin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old Mandarin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">
        Old Mandarin
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="793" data-file-width="543" height="248" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg/170px-Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg/255px-Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg/340px-Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mengu_Ziyun_xia_24b.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A page of the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Menggu_Ziyun" title="Menggu Ziyun">
           Menggu Ziyun
          </a>
         </i>
         , covering the syllables
         <i>
          tsim
         </i>
         to
         <i>
          lim
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       After the fall of the
       <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty#Northern_Song,_959–1126" title="Song dynasty">
        Northern Song
       </a>
       (959–1126) and during the reign of the
       <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">
        Jin
       </a>
       (1115–1234) and
       <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">
        Yuan
       </a>
       (Mongol) dynasties in northern China, a common speech developed based on the dialects of the North China Plain around the capital, a language referred to as Old Mandarin. New genres of vernacular literature were based on this language, including verse, drama and story forms, such as the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Qu_(poetry)" title="Qu (poetry)">
         qu
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Sanqu_poetry" title="Chinese Sanqu poetry">
         sanqu
        </a>
       </i>
       poetry.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198848–49-16">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The rhyming conventions of the new verse were codified in a
       <a href="/wiki/Rime_dictionary" title="Rime dictionary">
        rime dictionary
       </a>
       called the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Zhongyuan_Yinyun" title="Zhongyuan Yinyun">
         Zhongyuan Yinyun
        </a>
       </i>
       (1324). A radical departure from the
       <a href="/wiki/Rime_table" title="Rime table">
        rime table
       </a>
       tradition that had evolved over the previous centuries, this dictionary contains a wealth of information on the phonology of Old Mandarin. Further sources are the
       <a href="/wiki/%27Phags-pa_script" title="'Phags-pa script">
        'Phags-pa script
       </a>
       based on the Tibetan alphabet, which was used to write several of the languages of the Mongol empire, including Chinese, and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Menggu_Ziyun" title="Menggu Ziyun">
         Menggu Ziyun
        </a>
       </i>
       , a rime dictionary based on 'Phags-pa. The rime books differ in some details, but overall show many of the features characteristic of modern Mandarin dialects, such as the reduction and disappearance of final plosives and the reorganization of the Middle Chinese tones.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198849–51-17">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Middle Chinese, initial
       <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
        stops
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" title="Affricate consonant">
        affricates
       </a>
       showed a three-way contrast between
       <a href="/wiki/Tenuis_consonant" title="Tenuis consonant">
        tenuis
       </a>
       , voiceless aspirated and voiced consonants. There were
       <a href="/wiki/Four_tones_(Middle_Chinese)" title="Four tones (Middle Chinese)">
        four tones
       </a>
       , with the fourth, or "entering tone", a
       <a href="/wiki/Checked_tone" title="Checked tone">
        checked tone
       </a>
       comprising syllables ending in plosives (
       <i>
        -p
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        -t
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        -k
       </i>
       ). Syllables with voiced initials tended to be pronounced with a lower pitch, and by the late
       <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">
        Tang dynasty
       </a>
       , each of the tones had split into two registers conditioned by the initials. When voicing was lost in all languages except the Wu subfamily, this distinction became phonemic and the system of initials and tones was rearranged differently in each of the major groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198834–36,_52–54_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198834–36,_52–54-18">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Zhongyuan Yinyun
       </i>
       shows the typical Mandarin four-tone system resulting from a split of the "even" tone and loss of the entering tone, with its syllables distributed across the other tones (though their different origin is marked in the dictionary). Similarly, voiced plosives and affricates have become voiceless aspirates in the "even" tone and voiceless non-aspirates in others, another distinctive Mandarin development. However, the language still retained a final
       <i>
        -m
       </i>
       , which has merged with
       <i>
        -n
       </i>
       in modern dialects, and initial voiced fricatives. It also retained the distinction between velars and alveolar sibilants in palatal environments, which later merged in most Mandarin dialects to yield a palatal series (rendered
       <i>
        j-
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        q-
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        x-
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198849–50_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198849–50-19">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The flourishing vernacular literature of the period also shows distinctively Mandarin vocabulary and syntax, though some, such as the third-person pronoun
       <i>
        tā
       </i>
       (他), can be traced back to the Tang dynasty.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988111–132_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988111–132-20">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vernacular_literature">
        Vernacular literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vernacular literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Until the early 20th century, formal writing and even much poetry and fiction was done in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">
        Literary Chinese
       </a>
       , which was modeled on the
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_classics" title="Chinese classics">
        classics
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">
        Warring States period
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">
        Han dynasty
       </a>
       . Over time, the various spoken varieties diverged greatly from Literary Chinese, which was learned and composed as a special language. Preserved from the sound changes that affected the various spoken varieties, its economy of expression was greatly valued. For example,
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        翼
       </span>
       (
       <i>
        yì,
       </i>
       "wing") is unambiguous in written Chinese, but has over 75
       <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">
        homophones
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Standard Chinese
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The literary language was less appropriate for recording materials that were meant to be reproduced in oral presentations, materials such as plays and grist for the professional story-teller's mill. From at least the Yuan dynasty, plays that recounted the subversive tales of China's Robin Hoods to the Ming dynasty novels such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Water_Margin" title="Water Margin">
         Water Margin
        </a>
       </i>
       , on down to the Qing dynasty novel
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Dream_of_the_Red_Chamber" title="Dream of the Red Chamber">
         Dream of the Red Chamber
        </a>
       </i>
       and beyond, there developed a literature in
       <a href="/wiki/Written_vernacular_Chinese" title="Written vernacular Chinese">
        written vernacular Chinese
       </a>
       (白话/白話
       <i>
        báihuà
       </i>
       ). In many cases, this written language reflected Mandarin varieties, and since pronunciation differences were not conveyed in this written form, this tradition had a unifying force across all the Mandarin-speaking regions and beyond.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198710_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198710-21">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">
        Hu Shih
       </a>
       , a pivotal figure of the first half of the twentieth century, wrote an influential and perceptive study of this literary tradition, entitled
       <i>
        Báihuà Wénxuéshǐ
       </i>
       ("A History of Vernacular Literature").
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Koin.C3.A9_of_the_Late_Empire">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Koiné_of_the_Late_Empire">
        Koiné of the Late Empire
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Koiné of the Late Empire">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(late_imperial_lingua_franca)" title="Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)">
        Mandarin (late imperial lingua franca)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="256" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png/170px-Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png/255px-Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fourmont-Zhongguo-Guanhua.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Zhongguo Guanhua
         </i>
         (中國官話), or
         <i>
          Medii Regni Communis Loquela
         </i>
         ("Middle Kingdom's Common Speech"), used on the frontispiece of an early Chinese grammar published by
         <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Fourmont" title="Étienne Fourmont">
          Étienne Fourmont
         </a>
         (with
         <a href="/wiki/Arcadio_Huang" title="Arcadio Huang">
          Arcadio Huang
         </a>
         ) in 1742
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
          <a href="#cite_note-22">
           [21]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <blockquote class="quote-frame pullquote toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
       <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
        The Chinese have different languages in different provinces, to such an extent that they cannot understand each other.... [They] also have another language which is like a universal and common language; this is the official language of the mandarins and of the court; it is among them like Latin among ourselves.... Two of our fathers [Michele Ruggieri and Matteo Ricci] have been learning this mandarin language...
        <cite style="display: block; text-align: right;">
         —
         <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Valignano" title="Alessandro Valignano">
          Alessandro Valignano
         </a>
         ,
         <i>
          Historia del principio y progresso de la Compañía de Jesús en las Indias Orientales (1542–1564)
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000539_23-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000539-23">
            [22]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </i>
        </cite>
       </div>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Until the mid-20th century, most Chinese people living in many parts of
       <a href="/wiki/South_China" title="South China">
        South China
       </a>
       spoke only their local variety. As a practical measure, officials of the Ming and Qing dynasties carried out the administration of the empire using a common language based on Mandarin varieties, known as
       <i>
        Guānhuà
       </i>
       . Knowledge of this language was thus essential for an official career, but it was never formally defined.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988136_12-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988136-12">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Officials varied widely in their pronunciation; in 1728, the
       <a href="/wiki/Yongzheng_Emperor" title="Yongzheng Emperor">
        Yongzheng Emperor
       </a>
       , unable to understand the accents of officials from
       <a href="/wiki/Guangdong" title="Guangdong">
        Guangdong
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">
        Fujian
       </a>
       , issued a decree requiring the governors of those provinces to provide for the teaching of proper pronunciation. Although the resulting Academies for Correct Pronunciation (
       <span lang="zh" xml:lang="zh">
        正音書院
       </span>
       ,
       <i>
        Zhèngyīn Shūyuàn
       </i>
       ) were short-lived, the decree did spawn a number of textbooks that give some insight into the ideal pronunciation. Common features included:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        loss of the Middle Chinese voiced initials except for
        <i>
         v-
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        merger of
        <i>
         -m
        </i>
        finals with
        <i>
         -n
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        the characteristic Mandarin four-tone system in open syllables, but retaining a final glottal stop in "entering tone" syllables
       </li>
       <li>
        retention of the distinction between
        <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
         palatalized
        </a>
        velars and dental affricates, the source of the spellings "Peking" and "Tientsin" for modern "Beijing" and "Tianjin".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKaske200848–52_24-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaske200848–52-24">
          [23]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       As the last two of these features indicate, this language was a
       <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">
        koiné
       </a>
       based on dialects spoken in the
       <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">
        Nanjing
       </a>
       area, though not identical to any single dialect.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2003353_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2003353-25">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This form remained prestigious long after the capital moved to
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       in 1421, though the speech of the new capital emerged as a rival standard. As late as 1815,
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Morrison_(missionary)" title="Robert Morrison (missionary)">
        Robert Morrison
       </a>
       based the
       <a href="/wiki/A_Dictionary_of_the_Chinese_Language" title="A Dictionary of the Chinese Language">
        first English–Chinese dictionary
       </a>
       on this koiné as the standard of the time, though he conceded that the Beijing dialect was gaining in influence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the middle of the 19th century, the Beijing dialect had become dominant and was essential for any business with the imperial court.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoblin2000540–541-27">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Chinese">
        Standard Chinese
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Standard Chinese">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
        Standard Chinese
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the early years of the Republic of China, intellectuals of the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Culture_Movement" title="New Culture Movement">
        New Culture Movement
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Hu_Shih" title="Hu Shih">
        Hu Shih
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chen_Duxiu" title="Chen Duxiu">
        Chen Duxiu
       </a>
       , successfully campaigned for the replacement of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Chinese" title="Literary Chinese">
        Literary Chinese
       </a>
       as the written standard by
       <a href="/wiki/Written_vernacular_Chinese" title="Written vernacular Chinese">
        written vernacular Chinese
       </a>
       , which was based on northern dialects. A parallel priority was the definition of a standard national language (
       <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">
        simplified Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
        国语
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">
        traditional Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">
        國語
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">
        pinyin
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin" xml:lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">
         Guóyǔ
        </span>
       </i>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">
        Wade–Giles
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile" xml:lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">
         Kuo²-yü³
        </span>
       </i>
       ). After much dispute between proponents of northern and southern dialects and an abortive attempt at
       <a href="/wiki/Old_National_Pronunciation" title="Old National Pronunciation">
        an artificial pronunciation
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Languages_Committee" title="National Languages Committee">
        National Language Unification Commission
       </a>
       finally settled on the Beijing dialect in 1932. The People's Republic, founded in 1949, retained this standard, calling it
       <i>
        pǔtōnghuà
       </i>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">
        simplified Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hans" xml:lang="zh-Hans">
        普通话
       </span>
       ;
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">
        traditional Chinese
       </a>
       :
       <span lang="zh-Hant" xml:lang="zh-Hant">
        普通話
       </span>
       ; literally: "common speech").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey19873–15-28">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some 54% of speakers of Mandarin varieties could understand the standard language in the early 1950s, rising to 91% in 1984. Nationally, the proportion understanding the standard rose from 41% to 90% over the same period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen199927–28_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen199927–28-29">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The national language is now used in education, the media and formal occasions in both the PRC and the ROC but not in
       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">
        Hong Kong
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
        Macau
       </a>
       . This standard can now be spoken intelligibly by most younger people in
       <a href="/wiki/Mainland_China" title="Mainland China">
        Mainland China
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">
        Taiwan
       </a>
       with various regional accents. In
       <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">
        Hong Kong
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
        Macau
       </a>
       , because of their colonial and linguistic history, the sole language of education, the media, formal speech and everyday life remains the local
       <a href="/wiki/Cantonese" title="Cantonese">
        Cantonese
       </a>
       . Mandarin is now common and taught in many schools
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEZhangYang2004-30">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but still hasn't gained ground. In Mandarin-speaking areas such as Sichuan and Chongqing, the local dialect is the native tongue of most of the population.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
          <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (October 2012)">
           clarification needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The era of mass education in Standard Chinese has not erased these regional differences, and people may be either
       <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">
        diglossic
       </a>
       or speak the standard language with a notable accent.
      </p>
      <p>
       From an official point of view, the PRC and ROC governments maintain their own forms of the standard under different names. Technically, both
       <i>
        Pǔtōnghuà
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Guóyǔ
       </i>
       base their
       <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">
        phonology
       </a>
       on the Beijing accent, though
       <i>
        Pǔtōnghuà
       </i>
       also takes some elements from other sources. Comparison of dictionaries produced in the two areas will show that there are few substantial differences. However, both versions of "school-standard" Chinese are often quite different from the Mandarin varieties that are spoken in accordance with regional habits, and neither is wholly identical to the
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">
        Beijing dialect
       </a>
       .
       <i>
        Pǔtōnghuà
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Guóyǔ
       </i>
       also have some differences from the Beijing dialect in vocabulary, grammar, and
       <a href="/wiki/Pragmatics" title="Pragmatics">
        pragmatics
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The written forms of Standard Chinese are also essentially equivalent, although
       <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">
        simplified characters
       </a>
       are used in China, Singapore and Malaysia, while people in Hong Kong, Macau and Taiwan generally use
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">
        traditional characters
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution_and_dialects">
        Geographic distribution and dialects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution and dialects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_varieties_of_Chinese" title="List of varieties of Chinese">
        List of varieties of Chinese
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:366px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="728" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png/364px-Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png/546px-Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1d/Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png 2x" width="364"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandarin_subgroups_and_Jin_group.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Distribution of the eight subgroups of Mandarin plus
         <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">
          Jin Chinese
         </a>
         , which many linguists include as part of Mandarin, according to the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">
           Language Atlas of China
          </a>
         </i>
         (1987)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_A2_31-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_A2-31">
           [30]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Most Han Chinese living in northern and southwestern China are native speakers of a dialect of Mandarin. The
       <a href="/wiki/North_China_Plain" title="North China Plain">
        North China Plain
       </a>
       provided few barriers to migration, leading to relative linguistic homogeneity over a wide area in northern China. In contrast, the mountains and rivers of southern China have spawned the other six major groups of Chinese varieties, with great internal diversity, particularly in
       <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">
        Fujian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–190_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988183–190-32">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198722_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198722-33">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the varieties of Mandarin cover a huge area containing nearly a billion people. As a result, there are pronounced regional variations in
       <a href="/wiki/Pronunciation" title="Pronunciation">
        pronunciation
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">
        vocabulary
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">
        grammar
       </a>
       , and many Mandarin varieties are not mutually intelligible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [b]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most of northeastern China, except for
       <a href="/wiki/Liaoning" title="Liaoning">
        Liaoning
       </a>
       , did not receive significant settlements by Han Chinese until the 18th century,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichards2003138–139_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichards2003138–139-39">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and as a result the
       <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mandarin" title="Northeastern Mandarin">
        Northeastern Mandarin
       </a>
       dialects spoken there differ little from the
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">
        Beijing dialect
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198721_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198721-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">
        Manchu people
       </a>
       of the area now speak these dialects exclusively; their native language is only maintained in northwestern
       <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">
        Xinjiang
       </a>
       , where
       <a href="/wiki/Xibe_language" title="Xibe language">
        Xibe
       </a>
       , a modern dialect, is spoken.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey1987215–216_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey1987215–216-41">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The frontier areas of Northwest China were colonized by speakers of Mandarin dialects at the same time, and the dialects in those areas similarly closely resemble their relatives in the core Mandarin area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198721_40-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198721-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Southwest was settled early, but the population fell dramatically for obscure reasons in the 13th century, and did not recover until the 17th century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198721_40-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198721-40">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The dialects in this area are now relatively uniform.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, long-established cities even very close to
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       , such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">
        Tianjin
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Baoding" title="Baoding">
        Baoding
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Shenyang" title="Shenyang">
        Shenyang
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Dalian" title="Dalian">
        Dalian
       </a>
       , have markedly different dialects.
      </p>
      <p>
       Unlike their compatriots on the southeast coast, few Mandarin speakers engaged in
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_emigration" title="Chinese emigration">
        overseas emigration
       </a>
       until the late 20th century, but there are now significant communities of them in cities across the world.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">
        Classification
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classification">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (July 2011)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The classification of Chinese dialects evolved during the 20th century, and many points remain unsettled. Early classifications tended to follow provincial boundaries or major geographical features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201036–41_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201036–41-43">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1936,
       <a href="/wiki/Wang_Li_(linguist)" title="Wang Li (linguist)">
        Wang Li
       </a>
       produced the first classification based on phonetic criteria, principally the evolution of
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">
        Middle Chinese
       </a>
       voiced initials. His Mandarin group included dialects of northern and southwestern China, as well as those of
       <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">
        Hunan
       </a>
       and northern
       <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">
        Jiangxi
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–42_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–42-44">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Li_Fang-Kuei" title="Li Fang-Kuei">
        Li Fang-Kuei
       </a>
       's classification of 1937 distinguished the latter two groups as
       <a href="/wiki/Xiang_Chinese" title="Xiang Chinese">
        Xiang
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Gan_Chinese" title="Gan Chinese">
        Gan
       </a>
       , while splitting the remaining Mandarin dialects between Northern, Lower Yangtze and Southwestern Mandarin groups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201049_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201049-45">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The widely accepted seven-group classification of
       <a href="/wiki/Yuan_Jiahua" title="Yuan Jiahua">
        Yuan Jiahua
       </a>
       in 1960 kept Xiang and Gan separate, with Mandarin divided into Northern, Northwestern, Southwestern and Jiang–Huai (Lower Yangtze) subgroups.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–54_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201053–54-46">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988181,_191_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988181,_191-47">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Of Yuan's four Mandarin subgroups, the Northwestern dialects are the most diverse, particularly in the province of
       <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">
        Shanxi
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_42-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The linguist
       <a href="/wiki/Li_Rong_(linguist)" title="Li Rong (linguist)">
        Li Rong
       </a>
       proposed that the northwestern dialects of Shanxi and neighbouring areas that retain a final glottal stop in the Middle Chinese
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entering_tone" title="Entering tone">
        entering tone
       </a>
       (plosive-final) category should constitute a separate top-level group called
       <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">
        Jin
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYan200661_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYan200661-48">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He used this classification in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Language_Atlas_of_China" title="Language Atlas of China">
         Language Atlas of China
        </a>
       </i>
       (1987).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987-14">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many other linguists continue to include these dialects in the Mandarin group, pointing out that the Lower Yangtze dialects also retain the glottal stop.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETing1991190_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETing1991190-49">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56,_74–75_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055–56,_74–75-50">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The southern boundary of the Mandarin area, with the central
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">
        Wu
       </a>
       , Gan and Xiang groups, is weakly defined due to centuries of diffusion of northern features. Many border varieties have a mixture of features that make them difficult to classify. The boundary between Southwestern Mandarin and Xiang is particularly weak,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988190_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988190-51">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and in many early classifications the two were not separated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–46_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201041–46-52">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Zhenhe" title="Zhou Zhenhe">
        Zhou Zhenhe
       </a>
       and You Rujie include the
       <a href="/wiki/New_Xiang" title="New Xiang">
        New Xiang
       </a>
       dialects within Southwestern Mandarin, treating only the more conservative
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Xiang" title="Old Xiang">
        Old Xiang
       </a>
       dialects as a separate group.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201055-53">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Huizhou_Chinese" title="Huizhou Chinese">
        Huizhou
       </a>
       dialects have features of both Mandarin and Wu, and have been assigned to one or other of these groups or treated as separate by various authors. Li Rong and the
       <i>
        Language Atlas of China
       </i>
       treated it as a separate top-level group, but this remains controversial.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201075–76_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201075–76-54">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYan2006222–223_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYan2006222–223-55">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Language Atlas of China
       </i>
       calls the remainder of Mandarin a "supergroup", divided into eight dialect groups distinguished by their treatment of the Middle Chinese entering tone (see
       <a href="#Tones">
        Tones
       </a>
       below):
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201075_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201075-56">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mandarin" title="Northeastern Mandarin">
         Northeastern Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">
         Manchuria
        </a>
        except the
        <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">
         Liaodong Peninsula
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B1_57-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B1-57">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This dialect is closely related to Standard Chinese, with little variation in lexicon and very few tonal differences.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beijing_Mandarin" title="Beijing Mandarin">
         Beijing Mandarin
        </a>
        in Beijing and environs such as
        <a href="/wiki/Chengde" title="Chengde">
         Chengde
        </a>
        and northern
        <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">
         Hebei
        </a>
        , as well as some areas of recent large-scale immigration, such as northern
        <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">
         Xinjiang
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B2,_B5_58-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B2,_B5-58">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        The Beijing dialect forms the basis of Standard Chinese.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Jilu_Mandarin" title="Jilu Mandarin">
         Jilu Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">
         Hebei
        </a>
        ("Ji") and
        <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">
         Shandong
        </a>
        ("Lu") provinces except the
        <a href="/wiki/Shandong_Peninsula" title="Shandong Peninsula">
         Shandong Peninsula
        </a>
        , including
        <a href="/wiki/Tianjin_dialect" title="Tianjin dialect">
         Tianjin dialect
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B2_59-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B2-59">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Tones and vocabulary are markedly different. In general, there is substantial intelligibility with Beijing Mandarin.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Jiaoliao_Mandarin" title="Jiaoliao Mandarin">
         Jiaoliao Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Shandong_Peninsula" title="Shandong Peninsula">
         Shandong
        </a>
        (Jiaodong) and
        <a href="/wiki/Liaodong_Peninsula" title="Liaodong Peninsula">
         Liaodong Peninsulas
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B1,_B3_60-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B1,_B3-60">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Very noticeable tonal changes, different in "flavour" from Ji–Lu Mandarin, but with more variance. There is moderate intelligibility with Beijing.
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Central_Plains_Mandarin" title="Central Plains Mandarin">
         Central Plains Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Henan" title="Henan">
         Henan
        </a>
        province, the central parts of
        <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">
         Shaanxi
        </a>
        in the
        <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">
         Yellow River
        </a>
        valley, eastern
        <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">
         Gansu
        </a>
        and southern
        <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">
         Xinjiang
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B3,_B4,_B5_61-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B3,_B4,_B5-61">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
        There are significant phonological differences, with partial intelligibility with Beijing. The
        <a href="/wiki/Dungan_language" title="Dungan language">
         Dungan language
        </a>
        spoken in
        <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">
         Kazakhstan
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">
         Kyrgyzstan
        </a>
        belongs to this group. Dungan speakers such as the poet
        <a href="/wiki/Iasyr_Shivaza" title="Iasyr Shivaza">
         Iasyr Shivaza
        </a>
        have reported being understood by speakers of the Beijing dialect, but not vice versa.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERimsky-Korsakoff_Dyer1977–78351_62-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERimsky-Korsakoff_Dyer1977–78351-62">
          [60]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lanyin_Mandarin" title="Lanyin Mandarin">
         Lanyin Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in central and western
        <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">
         Gansu
        </a>
        province (with capital
        <a href="/wiki/Lanzhou" title="Lanzhou">
         Lanzhou
        </a>
        ) and
        <a href="/wiki/Ningxia" title="Ningxia">
         Ningxia
        </a>
        autonomous region (with capital
        <a href="/wiki/Yinchuan" title="Yinchuan">
         Yinchuan
        </a>
        ), as well as northern
        <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">
         Xinjiang
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B4,_B5_63-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B4,_B5-63">
          [61]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Lower_Yangtze_Mandarin" title="Lower Yangtze Mandarin">
         Lower Yangtze Mandarin
        </a>
        (or Jiang–Huai), spoken in the parts of
        <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">
         Jiangsu
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Anhui" title="Anhui">
         Anhui
        </a>
        on the north bank of the
        <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">
         Yangtze
        </a>
        , as well as some areas on the south bank, such as
        <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">
         Nanjing
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">
         Jiangsu
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Jiujiang" title="Jiujiang">
         Jiujiang
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">
         Jiangxi
        </a>
        , etc.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B3_64-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Map_B3-64">
          [62]
         </a>
        </sup>
        There are significant phonological and lexical changes to varying degrees, and intelligibility with Beijing is limited. Lower Yangtze Mandarin has been significantly influenced by
        <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">
         Wu Chinese
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Mandarin" title="Southwestern Mandarin">
         Southwestern Mandarin
        </a>
        , spoken in the provinces of
        <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">
         Hubei
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">
         Sichuan
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Guizhou" title="Guizhou">
         Guizhou
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">
         Yunnan
        </a>
        , and the Mandarin-speaking areas of
        <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">
         Hunan
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">
         Guangxi
        </a>
        and southern
        <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">
         Shaanxi
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B4,_B6_65-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWurmLiBaumannLee1987Maps_B4,_B6-65">
          [63]
         </a>
        </sup>
        There are sharp phonological, lexical, and tonal changes, and intelligibility with Beijing is limited to varying degrees.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEscure1997144_36-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEscure1997144-36">
          [35]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlum200127_37-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlum200127-37">
          [36]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Atlas
       </i>
       also includes several unclassified Mandarin dialects spoken in scattered pockets across southeastern China, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Nanping" title="Nanping">
        Nanping
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">
        Fujian
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Dongfang,_Hainan" title="Dongfang, Hainan">
        Dongfang
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Hainan" title="Hainan">
        Hainan
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201067–68_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKurpaska201067–68-66">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Another Mandarin variety of uncertain classification is apparently
       <a href="/wiki/Gyami" title="Gyami">
        Gyami
       </a>
       , recorded in the 19th century in the Tibetan foothills, who the Chinese apparently did not recognize as Chinese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair19905–6_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair19905–6-67">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">
        Phonology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Phonology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese_phonology" title="Standard Chinese phonology">
        Standard Chinese phonology
       </a>
      </div>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (July 2011)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">
        Syllables
       </a>
       consist maximally of an initial consonant, a glide, a vowel, a final, and tone. Not every syllable that is possible according to this rule actually exists in Mandarin, as there are rules prohibiting certain phonemes from appearing with others, and in practice there are only a few hundred distinct syllables.
      </p>
      <p>
       Phonological features that are generally shared by the Mandarin dialects include:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        the
        <a href="/wiki/Palatalization_(sound_change)" title="Palatalization (sound change)">
         palatalization
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">
         velar consonants
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">
         alveolar sibilants
        </a>
        when they occur before
        <a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant" title="Palatal approximant">
         palatal glides
        </a>
        ;
       </li>
       <li>
        one syllable contains maximum four phonemes (maximum three vowels and no consonant cluster)
       </li>
       <li>
        the disappearance of final
        <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" title="Stop consonant">
         stop consonants
        </a>
        and /-m/ (although in many Lower Yangtze Mandarin and
        <a href="/wiki/Jin_Chinese" title="Jin Chinese">
         Jin Chinese
        </a>
        dialects, an echo of the final stops is preserved as a
        <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
         glottal stop
        </a>
        );
       </li>
       <li>
        the reduction of the six
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
        tones inherited from
        <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">
         Middle Chinese
        </a>
        after the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tone_split" title="Tone split">
         tone split
        </a>
        to four tones;
       </li>
       <li>
        the presence of
        <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_consonant" title="Retroflex consonant">
         retroflex consonants
        </a>
        (although these are absent in many
        <a href="/wiki/Southwestern_Mandarin" title="Southwestern Mandarin">
         Southwestern
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_Mandarin" title="Northeastern Mandarin">
         Northeastern Mandarin
        </a>
        dialects);
       </li>
       <li>
        the historical
        <a href="/wiki/Consonant_voicing_and_devoicing" title="Consonant voicing and devoicing">
         devoicing
        </a>
        of stops and sibilants (also common to most non-Mandarin varieties).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Initials">
        Initials
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Initials">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The maximal inventory of initials of a Mandarin dialect is as follows, with bracketed pinyin spellings given for those present in the standard language:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988139–141,_192_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988139–141,_192-68">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

       <tr>
        <th>
