       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Fricative_consonant" title="Fricative consonant">
          Fricatives
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /f/
         </span>
         ⟨f⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /s/
         </span>

        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ʂ/
         </span>
         ⟨sh⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɕ/
         </span>
         ⟨x⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /x/
         </span>
         ⟨h⟩
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         <a href="/wiki/Sonorant" title="Sonorant">
          Sonorants
         </a>
        </th>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /w/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /l/
         </span>
         ⟨l⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /ɻ ~ ʐ/
         </span>
         ⟨r⟩
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          /j/
         </span>
        </td>
        <td>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Most Mandarin-speaking areas distinguish between the retroflex initials
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʈ͡ʂ ʈ͡ʂʰ ʂ/
        </span>
        from the apical sibilants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ts tsʰ s/
        </span>
        , though they often have a different distribution than in the standard language. In most dialects of the southeast and southwest the retroflex initials have merged with the alveolar sibilants, so that
        <i>
         zhi
        </i>
        becomes
        <i>
         zi
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         chi
        </i>
        becomes
        <i>
         ci
        </i>
        , and
        <i>
         shi
        </i>
        becomes
        <i>
         si
        </i>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        The alveolo-palatal sibilants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tɕ tɕʰ ɕ/
        </span>
        are the result of merger between the historical palatalized velars
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /kj kʰj xj/
        </span>
        and palatalized alveolar sibilants
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tsj tsʰj sj/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        In about 20% of dialects, the alveolar sibilants failed to palatalize, remaining separate from the alveolo-palatal initials. (The unique pronunciation used in
        <a href="/wiki/Peking_opera" title="Peking opera">
         Peking opera
        </a>
        falls into this category.) On the other side, in some dialects of eastern
        <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">
         Shandong
        </a>
        , the velar initials have failed to palatalize.
       </li>
       <li>
        Many southwestern Mandarin dialects mix
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /f/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /xw/
        </span>
        , substituting one for the other in some or all cases.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988192_70-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988192-70">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
        For example,
        <i>
         fei
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /fei/
        </span>
        "to fly" and
        <i>
         hui
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /xwei/
        </span>
        "dust" may be merged in these areas.
       </li>
       <li>
        In some dialects, initial
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        are not distinguished. In Southwestern Mandarin, these sounds usually merge to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        ; in Lower Yangtze Mandarin, they usually merge to
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988192_70-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988192-70">
          [68]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        People in many Mandarin-speaking areas may use different initial sounds where Beijing uses initial
        <i>
         r-
        </i>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɻ/
        </span>
        . Common variants include
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /j
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /l/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /n/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /w/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        Some dialects have initial
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ŋ/
        </span>
        corresponding to the zero initial of the standard language.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        This initial is the result of a merger of the Middle Chinese zero initial with
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ŋ/
        </span>
        and
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʔ/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Many dialects of Northwestern and Central Plains Mandarin have
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /pf pfʰ f v/
        </span>
        where Beijing has
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʂw tʂʰw ʂw ɻw/
        </span>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
          [67]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Examples include
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /pfu/
        </span>
        "pig" for standard
        <i>
         zhū
        </i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B1%AC" title="wikt:豬">
         豬
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /tʂu/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /fei/
        </span>
        "water" for standard
        <i>
         shuǐ
        </i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%B0%B4" title="wikt:水">
         水
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ʂwei/
        </span>
        ,
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /vã/
        </span>
        "soft" for standard
        <i>
         ruǎn
        </i>
        <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BB%9F" title="wikt:軟">
         軟
        </a>
        <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
         /ɻwan/
        </span>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Finals">
        Finals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Finals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Most Mandarin dialects have three medial glides,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       ,
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɥ/
       </span>
       (spelled
       <i>
        i
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        u
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ü
       </i>
       in pinyin), though their incidence varies. The medial
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       , is lost after apical initials in several areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Thus Southwestern Mandarin has
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tei/
       </span>
       "right" where the standard language has
       <i>
        dui
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /twei/
       </span>
       . Southwestern Mandarin also has
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /kai kʰai xai/
       </span>
       in some words where the standard has
       <i>
        jie qie xie
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tɕjɛ tɕʰjɛ ɕjɛ/
       </span>
       . This is a stereotypical feature of southwestern Mandarin, since it is so easily noticeable. E.g.
       <i>
        hai
       </i>
       "shoe" for standard
       <i>
        xie
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        gai
       </i>
       "street" for standard
       <i>
        jie
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Mandarin dialects typically have relatively few vowels.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syllabic_fricative" title="Syllabic fricative">
        Syllabic fricatives
       </a>
       , as in standard
       <i>
        zi
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        zhi
       </i>
       , are common in Mandarin dialects, though they also occur elsewhere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988194_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988194-71">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Middle Chinese off-glides
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /w/
       </span>
       are generally preserved in Mandarin dialects, yielding several
       <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">
        diphthongs
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Triphthong" title="Triphthong">
        triphthongs
       </a>
       in contrast to the larger sets of monophthongs common in other dialect groups (and some widely scattered Mandarin dialects).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988194_71-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988194-71">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Middle Chinese coda
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /m/
       </span>
       was still present in
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">
        Old Mandarin
       </a>
       , but has merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       in the modern dialects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In some areas (especially the southwest) final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ŋ/
       </span>
       has also merged with
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       . This is especially prevalent in the rhyme pairs
       <i>
        -en/-eng
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ən əŋ/
       </span>
       and
       <i>
        -in/-ing
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /in iŋ/
       </span>
       . As a result,
       <i>
        jīn
       </i>
       "gold" and
       <i>
        jīng
       </i>
       "capital" merge in those dialects.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Middle Chinese final stops have undergone a variety of developments in different Mandarin dialects (see
       <a href="#Tones">
        Tones
       </a>
       below). In Lower Yangtze dialects and some north-western dialects they have merged as a final
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       . In other dialects they have been lost, with varying effects on the vowel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988193_69-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988193-69">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a result, Beijing Mandarin and Northeastern Mandarin underwent more vowel mergers than many other varieties of Mandarin. For example:
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; margin:1em auto 1em auto">
       <tr>

         </span>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">

      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/R-colored_vowel" title="R-colored vowel">
        R-coloring
       </a>
       , a characteristic feature of Mandarin, works quite differently in the southwest. Whereas Beijing dialect generally removes only a final
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /j/
       </span>
       or
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /n/
       </span>
       when adding the rhotic final
       <i>
        -r
       </i>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ɻ/
       </span>
       , in the southwest the
       <i>
        -r
       </i>
       replaces nearly the entire rhyme.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tones">
        Tones
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Tones">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="88" data-file-width="448" height="43" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg/220px-Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg/330px-Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg/440px-Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mandarin_tones_in_musical_notation.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Four tones of
         <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">
          Modern Standard Mandarin Chinese
         </a>
         , using musical notation.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In general, no two Mandarin-speaking areas have exactly the same set of
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_(linguistics)" title="Tone (linguistics)">
        tone
       </a>
       values, but most Mandarin-speaking areas have very similar tone
       <i>
        distribution
       </i>
       . For example, the dialects of
       <a href="/wiki/Jinan" title="Jinan">
        Jinan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chengdu" title="Chengdu">
        Chengdu
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">
        Xi'an
       </a>
       and so on all have four tones that correspond quite well to the
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing_dialect" title="Beijing dialect">
        Beijing dialect
       </a>
       tones of
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [˥]
       </span>
       (55),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [˧˥]
       </span>
       (35),
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [˨˩˦]
       </span>
       (214), and
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        [˥˩]
       </span>
       (51). The exception to this rule lies in the distribution of syllables formerly ending in a stop consonant, which are treated differently in different dialects of Mandarin.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988194–196_73-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988194–196-73">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Middle_Chinese" title="Middle Chinese">
        Middle Chinese
       </a>
       stops and affricates had a three-way distinction between tenuis, voiceless aspirate and voiced (or breathy voiced) consonants. In Mandarin dialects the voicing is generally lost, yielding voiceless aspirates in syllables with a Middle Chinese level tone and non-aspirates in other syllables.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988191_42-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988191-42">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Four_tones" title="Four tones">
        four tones
       </a>
       of Middle Chinese, the level, rising and departing tones have also developed into four modern tones in a uniform way across Mandarin dialects: the Middle Chinese level tone has split into two registers, conditioned on voicing of the Middle Chinese initial, while rising tone syllables with voiced obstruent initials have shifted to the departing tone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988194–195_74-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988194–195-74">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The following examples from the standard language illustrate the regular development common to Mandarin dialects (recall that pinyin
       <i>
        d
       </i>
       denotes a non-aspirate
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /t/
       </span>
       , while
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       denotes an aspirate
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /tʰ/
       </span>
       ):
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; margin:1em auto 1em auto">
       <caption>
        Reflexes of Middle Chinese initials and tones in modern Mandarin
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Middle Chinese tone
        </th>
        <td colspan="4">
         "level tone"
         <br/>
         (
         <i>
          píng
         </i>
         平)
        </td>
        <td colspan="4">
         "rising tone"
         <br/>
         (
         <i>
          shǎng
         </i>
         上)
        </td>
        <td colspan="4">
         "departing tone"
         <br/>
         (
         <i>
          qù
         </i>
         去)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th>
         Example
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%B8%B9" title="wikt:丹">
          丹
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%81%98" title="wikt:灘">
          灘
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%98%AD" title="wikt:蘭">
          蘭
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%BD%88" title="wikt:彈">
          彈
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BA%B6" title="wikt:亶">
          亶
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E5%9D%A6" title="wikt:坦">
          坦
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%87%B6" title="wikt:懶">
          懶
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E4%BD%86" title="wikt:但">
          但
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%97%A6" title="wikt:旦">

      <p>
       In traditional Chinese phonology, syllables that ended in a stop in Middle Chinese (i.e. /p/, /t/ or /k/) were considered to belong to a special category known as the "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entering_tone" title="Entering tone">
        entering tone
       </a>
       ". These final stops have disappeared in most Mandarin dialects, with the syllables distributed over the other four modern tones in different ways in the various Mandarin subgroups.
      </p>
      <p>
       In the Beijing dialect that underlies the standard language, syllables beginning with original voiceless consonants were redistributed across the four tones in a completely random pattern.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988195_75-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988195-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, the three characters
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E7%A7%AF" title="wikt:积">
        积
       </a>
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%84%8A" title="wikt:脊">
        脊
       </a>
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%BF%B9" title="wikt:迹">
        迹
       </a>
       , all
       <i>
        tsjek
       </i>
       in Middle Chinese (
       <a href="/wiki/Baxter%27s_transcription_for_Middle_Chinese" title="Baxter's transcription for Middle Chinese">
        William H. Baxter's transcription
       </a>
       ), are now pronounced
       <i>
        jī
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        jǐ
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        jì
       </i>
       respectively. Older dictionaries such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mathews%27_Chinese-English_Dictionary" title="Mathews' Chinese-English Dictionary">
         Mathews' Chinese-English Dictionary
        </a>
       </i>
       mark characters whose pronunciation formerly ended with a stop with a superscript 5; however, this tone number is more commonly used for syllables that always have a neutral tone (see below).
      </p>
      <p>
       In Lower Yangtze dialects, a minority of Southwestern dialects (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Minjiang_dialect" title="Minjiang dialect">
        Minjiang
       </a>
       ) and Jin Chinese (sometimes considered non-Mandarin), former final stops were not deleted entirely, but were reduced to a
       <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">
        glottal stop
       </a>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        /ʔ/
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988195_75-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988195-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This development is shared with
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">
        Wu Chinese
       </a>
       and is thought to represent the pronunciation of
       <a href="/wiki/Old_Mandarin" title="Old Mandarin">
        Old Mandarin
       </a>
       . In line with traditional Chinese phonology, dialects such as Lower Yangtze and Minjiang are thus said to have five tones instead of four. However, modern linguistics considers these syllables as having no
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phonemic" title="Phonemic">
        phonemic
       </a>
       tone at all.
      </p>

      <p>
       Although the system of tones is common across Mandarin dialects, their realization as
       <a href="/wiki/Tone_contour" title="Tone contour">
        tone contours
       </a>
       varies widely:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988195–196_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988195–196-77">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

        </td>

         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˥
         </span>
         (55)
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˥ʔ
         </span>
         (5)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         <a href="/wiki/Nantong" title="Nantong">
          Nantong
         </a>
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˨˩
         </span>
         (21)
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˧˥
         </span>
         (35)
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˥
         </span>
         (55)
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˦˨
         </span>
         (42),
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˨˩˧
         </span>
         (213)*
        </td>
        <td>
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˦ʔ
         </span>
         (4),
         <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
          ˥ʔ
         </span>
         (5)*
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       * Dialects in and around the Nantong area typically have many more than 4 tones, due to influence from the neighbouring
       <a href="/wiki/Wu_Chinese" title="Wu Chinese">
        Wu dialects
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Mandarin dialects frequently employ neutral tones in the second syllables of words, creating syllables whose tone contour is so short and light that it is difficult or impossible to discriminate. These atonal syllables also occur in non-Mandarin dialects, but in many southern dialects the tones of all syllables are made clear.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988195_75-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988195-75">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">
        Vocabulary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There are more polysyllabic words in Mandarin than in all other major varieties of Chinese except
       <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">
        Shanghainese
       </a>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2011)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       . This is partly because Mandarin has undergone many more sound changes than have southern varieties of Chinese, and has needed to deal with many more
       <a href="/wiki/Homophone" title="Homophone">
        homophones
       </a>
       . New words have been formed by adding
       <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">
        affixes
       </a>
       such as
       <i>
        lao-
       </i>
       (老),
       <i>
        -zi
       </i>
       (子),
       <i>
        -(e)r
       </i>
       (儿/兒), and
       <i>
        -tou
       </i>
       (头/頭), or by compounding, e.g. by combining two words of similar meaning as in
       <i>
        cōngmáng
       </i>
       (匆忙), made from elements meaning "hurried" and "busy". A distinctive feature of southwestern Mandarin is its frequent use of noun
       <a href="/wiki/Reduplication" title="Reduplication">
        reduplication
       </a>
       , which is hardly used in Beijing. In
       <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">
        Sichuan
       </a>
       , one hears
       <i>
        bāobāo
       </i>
       (包包) "handbag" where Beijing uses
       <i>
        bāo'r
       </i>
       (包儿). There are also a small number of words that have been polysyllabic since Old Chinese, such as
       <i>
        húdié
       </i>
       (蝴蝶) "butterfly".
      </p>
      <p>
       The singular
       <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">
        pronouns
       </a>
       in Mandarin are
       <i>
        wǒ
       </i>
       (我) "I",
       <i>
        nǐ
       </i>
       (你 or 妳) "you",
       <i>
        nín
       </i>
       (您) "you (formal)", and
       <i>
        tā
       </i>
       (他, 她 or 它) "he/she/it", with
       <i>
        -men
       </i>
       (们們) added for the plural. Further, there is a distinction between the plural first-person pronoun
       <i>
        zánmen
       </i>
       (咱们/咱們), which is inclusive of the listener, and
       <i>
        wǒmen
       </i>
       (我们/我們), which may be exclusive of the listener. Dialects of Mandarin agree with each other quite consistently on these pronouns. While the first and second person singular pronouns are cognate with forms in other varieties of Chinese, the rest of the pronominal system is a Mandarin innovation (e.g.,
       <a href="/wiki/Shanghainese" title="Shanghainese">
        Shanghainese
       </a>
       has
       <i>
        non
       </i>
       侬/儂 "you" and
       <i>
        yi
       </i>
       伊 "he/she").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988182,_195–196_78-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988182,_195–196-78">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of contact with Mongolian and Manchurian peoples, Mandarin (especially the Northeastern varieties) has some loanwords from these languages not present in other varieties of Chinese, such as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hutong" title="Hutong">
         hútòng
        </a>
       </i>
       (胡同) "alley".
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Chinese_dialects" title="List of Chinese dialects">
        Southern Chinese
       </a>
       varieties have borrowed from
       <a href="/wiki/Tai_languages" title="Tai languages">
        Tai
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERamsey198736–38_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamsey198736–38-79">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Austroasiatic_languages" title="Austroasiatic languages">
        Austroasiatic
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">
        Austronesian languages
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also many Chinese words came from foreign languages such as
       <i>
        gāo ěr fū
       </i>
       (高尔夫) from golf;
       <i>
        bǐ jī ní
       </i>
       (比基尼) from bikini;
       <i>
        hàn bǎo bāo
       </i>
       (汉堡包) from hamburger.
      </p>
      <p>
       In general, the greatest variation occurs in slang, in kinship terms, in names for common crops and domesticated animals, for common verbs and adjectives, and other such everyday terms. The least variation occurs in "formal" vocabulary—terms dealing with science, law, or government.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">
        Grammar
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Grammar">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Chinese_grammar" title="Chinese grammar">
        Chinese grammar
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Chinese varieties of all periods have traditionally been considered prime examples of
       <a href="/wiki/Analytic_language" title="Analytic language">
        analytic languages
       </a>
       , relying on word order and particles instead of
       <a href="/wiki/Inflection" title="Inflection">
        inflection
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">
        affixes
       </a>
       to provide grammatical information such as
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">
        person
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">
        number
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">
        tense
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">
        mood
       </a>
       , or
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">
        case
       </a>
       . Although modern varieties, including the Mandarin dialects, use a small number of particles in a similar fashion to suffixes, they are still strongly analytic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman198810_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman198810-81">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The basic word order of
       <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93verb%E2%80%93object" title="Subject–verb–object">
        subject–verb–object
       </a>
       is common across Chinese dialects, but there are variations in the order of the two objects of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ditransitive" title="Ditransitive">
        ditransitive
       </a>
       sentences. In northern dialects the indirect object precedes the direct object (as in English), for example in the Standard Chinese sentence:
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <table style="border: 1px solid darkgray; text-align: center;">
        <tr>
         <td>
          我
         </td>
         <td>
          给
         </td>
         <td>
          你
         </td>
         <td>
          一本
         </td>
         <td>
          书 。
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td>
          <i>
           wǒ
          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           gěi
          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           nǐ
          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           yìběn
          </i>
         </td>
         <td>
          <i>
           shū
          </i>
          .
         </td>
        </tr>
        <tr>
         <td>
          I
         </td>
         <td>
          give
         </td>
         <td>
          you
         </td>
         <td>
          a (one)
         </td>
         <td>
          book.
         </td>
        </tr>
       </table>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       In southern dialects, as well as many southwestern and Lower Yangtze dialects, the objects occur in the reverse order.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988162_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988162-82">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue2003105–106_83-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue2003105–106-83">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most varieties of Chinese use post-verbal particles to indicate
       <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">
        aspect
       </a>
       , but the particles used vary. Most Mandarin dialects use the particle
       <i>
        -le
       </i>
       (了) to indicate the
       <a href="/wiki/Perfective_aspect" title="Perfective aspect">
        perfective aspect
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        -zhe
       </i>
       (着/著) for the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progressive_aspect" title="Progressive aspect">
        progressive aspect
       </a>
       . Other Chinese varieties tend to use different particles, e.g. Cantonese
       <i>
        zo
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </i>
       咗 and
       <i>
        gan
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </i>
       紧/緊 respectively. The experiential aspect particle
       <i>
        -guo
       </i>
       (过/過) is used more widely, except in Southern Min.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue200390–93_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue200390–93-84">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The subordinative particle
       <i>
        de
       </i>
       (的) is characteristic of Mandarin dialects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorman1988196_85-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorman1988196-85">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some southern dialects, and a few Lower Yangtze dialects, preserve an older pattern of subordination without a marking particle, while in others a
       <a href="/wiki/Classifier_(linguistics)" title="Classifier (linguistics)">
        classifier
       </a>
       fulfils the role of the Mandarin particle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYue2003113–115_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYue2003113–115-86">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Especially in conversational Chinese, sentence-final
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particle_(grammar)" title="Particle (grammar)">
        particles
       </a>
       alter the inherent meaning of a sentence. Like much vocabulary, particles can vary a great deal with regards to the locale. For example, the particle
       <i>
        ma
       </i>
       (嘛), which is used in most northern dialects to denote obviousness or contention, is replaced by
       <i>
        yo
       </i>
       (哟) in southern usage.
      </p>
      <p>
       Some characters in Mandarin can be combined with others to indicate a particular meaning just like prefix and suffix in English. For example, the suffix -er which means the person who is doing the action, e.g. teacher, person who teaches. In Mandarin the character 師 functions the same thing, it is combined with 教, which means teach, to form the word teacher.
      </p>
      <p>
       List of Chinese prefix and suffix
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable">
       <tr>
        <th>
         Affix
        </th>
        <th>
         Pronunciation
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning
        </th>
        <th>
         Example
        </th>
        <th>
         Meaning of Example
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         -們[们]
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          men
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         plural, same as -s, -es
        </td>
        <td>
         學生們 [学生们]、朋友們 [朋友們]
        </td>
        <td>
         students, friends
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>
         可-
        </td>
        <td>
         <i>
          kě
         </i>
        </td>
        <td>
         same as -able
        </td>
        <td>
         可信、可笑、可靠
        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td>

        </td>

        </td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
        <td>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

        </td>

        </td>

       <tr>

        <td>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mandarin_Chinese&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references">
         <li id="cite_note-10">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-10">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           A
           <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">
            folk etymology
           </a>
           deriving the name from
           <i>
            Mǎn dà rén
           </i>
           (满大人 "
           <a href="/wiki/Manchu_people" title="Manchu people">
            Manchu
           </a>
           big man") is without foundation.
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERazfarRumenapp2013293_9-0">
            <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERazfarRumenapp2013293-9">
             [9]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-38">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-38">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           For example:
          </span>
          <ul>
           <li>
            <span class="reference-text">
             In the early 1950s, only 54% of people in the Mandarin-speaking area could understand Standard Chinese, which was based on the Beijing dialect.
             <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChen199927_34-0">
              <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChen199927-34">
               [33]
              </a>
             </sup>
            </span>
           </li>
           <li>
            <span class="reference-text">
             "Hence we see that even Mandarin includes within it an unspecified number of languages, very few of which have ever been reduced to writing, that are mutually unintelligible."
             <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMair199118_35-0">
              <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMair199118-35">
               [34]
              </a>
             </sup>
            </span>
           </li>
           <li>
            <span class="reference-text">
             "the common term assigned by linguists to this group of languages implies a certain homogeneity which is more likely to be related to the sociopolitical context than to linguistic reality, since most of those varieties are not mutually intelligible."
             <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEscure1997144_36-0">
              <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEscure1997144-36">
               [35]
              </a>
             </sup>
            </span>
           </li>
           <li>
            <span class="reference-text">
             "A speaker of only standard Mandarin might take a week or two to comprehend even simple Kunminghua with ease—and then only if willing to learn it."
             <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlum200127_37-0">
              <a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlum200127-37">
               [36]
              </a>
             </sup>
            </span>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-72">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-72">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The development is purely due to the preservation of an early glide which later became
           <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
            /j/
           </span>
           and triggered patalization, and does not indicate the absence of a vowel merger.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
